<p><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Archimedes' principle" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archimedes%27_principle">Archimedes' principle</a></dfn>States that the upward&nbsp;<a title="Buoyancy" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buoyancy">buoyant force</a>&nbsp;that is exerted on a body immersed in a&nbsp;<a title="Fluid" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fluid">fluid</a>, whether fully or partially submerged, is equal to the&nbsp;<a title="Weight" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weight">weight</a>&nbsp;of the fluid that the body&nbsp;<a title="Displacement (fluid)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Displacement_(fluid)">displaces</a>&nbsp;and acts in the upward direction at the center of mass of the displaced fluid.</p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Area moment of inertia" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Area_moment_of_inertia">area moment of inertia</a></dfn></p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Astronomical unit" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_unit">astronomical unit</a></dfn>(Symbol:&nbsp;<strong>au</strong>,&nbsp;<strong>ua</strong>,&nbsp;or&nbsp;<strong>AU</strong>) is a&nbsp;<a title="Unit of length" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_of_length">unit of length</a>, roughly the distance from&nbsp;<a title="Earth" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth">Earth</a>&nbsp;to the&nbsp;<a title="Sun" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun">Sun</a>. However, that distance varies as Earth orbits the Sun, from a maximum (<a class="mw-redirect" title="Aphelion" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aphelion">aphelion</a>) to a minimum (<a class="mw-redirect" title="Perihelion" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perihelion">perihelion</a>) and back again once a year. Originally conceived as the average of Earth's aphelion and perihelion, since 2012 it has been defined as exactly 149,597,870,700 metres, or about 150&nbsp;million kilometres (93&nbsp;million miles).&nbsp;The astronomical unit is used primarily for measuring distances within the&nbsp;<a title="Solar System" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System">Solar System</a>&nbsp;or around other stars. It is also a fundamental component in the definition of another unit of astronomical length, the&nbsp;<a title="Parsec" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parsec">parsec</a>.</p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Astrophysics" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astrophysics">astrophysics</a></dfn>The branch of&nbsp;<a title="Astronomy" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomy">astronomy</a>&nbsp;that deals with the physics of the&nbsp;<a title="Universe" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universe">Universe</a>, especially with the nature of celestial bodies rather than their positions or motions in space.</p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Attenuation coefficient" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Attenuation_coefficient">attenuation coefficient</a></dfn></p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Atom" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atom">atom</a></dfn>A basic unit of&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#matter"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;matter">matter</span></a>&nbsp;that consists of a dense central nucleus surrounded by a cloud of negatively charged&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electron"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electron">electrons</span></a>. The atomic nucleus contains a mix of positively charged&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#proton"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;proton">protons</span></a>&nbsp;and electrically neutral&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#neutron"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;neutron">neutrons</span></a>.</p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Atomic line filter" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_line_filter">atomic line filter</a></dfn></p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Atomic mass" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_mass">atomic mass</a></dfn></p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic mass unit" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_mass_unit">atomic mass unit</a></dfn>1/12 of the mass of an&nbsp;unbound&nbsp;neutral atom of&nbsp;carbon-12&nbsp;in its nuclear and electronic&nbsp;ground state&nbsp;and&nbsp;at rest.</p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Atomic number" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_number">atomic number</a>&nbsp;(Z)</dfn>The number of&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#proton"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;proton">protons</span></a>&nbsp;found in the nucleus of an&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#atom"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;atom">atom</span></a>. It is most often used to classify elements within the&nbsp;<a title="Periodic table" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Periodic_table">periodic table</a>.</p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Atomic orbital" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_orbital">atomic orbital</a></dfn></p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Atomic packing factor" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_packing_factor">atomic packing factor</a></dfn></p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Atomic physics" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_physics">atomic physics</a></dfn>A branch of physics that studies&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#atom"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;atom">atoms</span></a>&nbsp;as isolated systems of&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electron"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electron">electrons</span></a>&nbsp;and an&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#nucleus"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;nucleus">atomic nucleus</span></a>. Compare&nbsp;<em><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#nuclear_physics"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;nuclear physics">nuclear physics</span></a></em>.</p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic structure" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_structure">atomic structure</a></dfn></p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic weight" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_weight">atomic weight</a>&nbsp;(A)</dfn>The sum total of&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#proton"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;proton">protons</span></a>&nbsp;(or&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electron"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electron">electrons</span></a>) and&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#neutron"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;neutron">neutrons</span></a>&nbsp;within an&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#atom"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;atom">atom</span></a>.</p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Audio frequency" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Audio_frequency">audio frequency</a></dfn></p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Speed" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed#Average_speed">average speed</a></dfn></p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Avogadro constant" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Avogadro_constant">Avogadro constant</a></dfn>(6.02214086 &times; 10<sup>23</sup> mol<sup>&minus;1</sup>) In chemistry and physics, the Avogadro constant (named after the scientist Amedeo Avogadro) is the number of constituent particles, usually atoms or molecules, that are contained in the amount of substance given by one mole.</p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Avogadro's law" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Avogadro%27s_law">Avogadro's law</a></dfn>A physical law which states that volumes of gases which are equal to each other at the same temperature and pressure will contain equal numbers of molecules.</p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Avogadro's number" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Avogadro%27s_number">Avogadro's number</a></dfn>The number of molecules in exactly 12g of carbon-12, equaling 6.022 x 10<sup>23</sup>.</p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Axion" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axion">axion</a></dfn>A hypothetical&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#subatomic_particle"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;subatomic particle">subatomic particle</span></a>&nbsp;postulated to account for the rarity of processes that break charge-parity symmetry. It is very light, electrically neutral, and pseudoscalar.</p>
<p><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Azimuthal quantum number" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Azimuthal_quantum_number">azimuthal quantum number</a></dfn></p>
<h2><span id="B" class="mw-headline">B</span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt id="babinet's_principle" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Babinet's principle" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Babinet%27s_principle">Babinet's principle</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A theorem concerning diffraction which states that the diffraction pattern from an opaque body is identical to that from a hole of the same size and shape except for the overall forward beam intensity.</dd>
<dt id="background_radiation" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Background radiation" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Background_radiation">background radiation</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The ubiquitous ionising radiation to which the general population is exposed.</dd>
<dt id="ballistics" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Ballistics" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ballistics">ballistics</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="balmer_series" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Balmer series" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balmer_series">Balmer series</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Or Balmer lines, in&nbsp;<a title="Atomic physics" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_physics">atomic physics</a>, is one of a set of&nbsp;<a title="Hydrogen spectral series" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen_spectral_series">six named series</a>&nbsp;describing the&nbsp;<a title="Spectral line" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spectral_line">spectral line</a>&nbsp;emissions of the&nbsp;<a title="Hydrogen atom" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen_atom">hydrogen atom</a>. The Balmer series is calculated using the Balmer formula, an&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Empirical" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Empirical">empirical</a>&nbsp;equation discovered by&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Johann Balmer" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Balmer">Johann Balmer</a>&nbsp;in 1885.</dd>
<dt id="barometer" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Barometer" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barometer">barometer</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A scientific instrument used in&nbsp;<a title="Meteorology" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meteorology">meteorology</a>&nbsp;to measure&nbsp;<a title="Atmospheric pressure" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure">atmospheric pressure</a>. Pressure tendency can forecast short-term changes in the weather.</dd>
<dt id="baryon" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Baryon" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baryon">baryon</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A&nbsp;<a title="Subatomic particle" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subatomic_particle">subatomic particle</a>&nbsp;such as a&nbsp;<a title="Proton" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proton">proton</a>&nbsp;or a&nbsp;<a title="Neutron" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutron">neutron</a>, made of (usually) three&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Quarks" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quarks">quarks</a>. Nearly all&nbsp;<a title="Matter" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Matter">matter</a>&nbsp;you are likely to encounter is baryonic matter.</dd>
<dt id="battery" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Battery (electricity)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battery_(electricity)">battery</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A combination of two or more electrical cells which produces&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electricity"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electricity">electricity</span></a>.</dd>
<dt id="beam" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Beam (structure)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beam_(structure)">beam</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A structural element that is capable of withstanding load primarily by resisting bending.Beams are traditionally descriptions of building or civil engineering structural elements, but smaller structures such as truck or automobile frames, machine frames, and other mechanical or structural systems contain beam structures that are designed and analyzed in a similar fashion.</dd>
<dt id="bending" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Bending" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bending">bending</a></dfn></dt>
</dl>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><span class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also known as&nbsp;<strong>flexure</strong>.</span></p>
<dl class="glossary">
<dd class="glossary">The behavior of a slender structural element subjected to an external load applied perpendicularly to a longitudinal axis of the element.</dd>
<dt id="bending_moment" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Bending moment" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bending_moment">bending moment</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Is the reaction induced in a&nbsp;<a title="Structural element" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Structural_element">structural element</a>&nbsp;when an external&nbsp;<a title="Force" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Force">force</a>&nbsp;or&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Moment of force" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moment_of_force">moment</a>&nbsp;is applied to the element causing the element to&nbsp;<a title="Bending" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bending">bend</a>.&nbsp;The most common or simplest structural element subjected to bending moments is the&nbsp;<a title="Beam (structure)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beam_(structure)">beam</a>.</dd>
<dt id="bernoulli_equation" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Bernoulli's equation" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bernoulli%27s_equation">Bernoulli equation</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="bernoulli's_principle" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Bernoulli's principle" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bernoulli%27s_principle">Bernoulli's principle</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In&nbsp;<a title="Fluid dynamics" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fluid_dynamics">fluid dynamics</a>, Bernoulli's principle states that an increase in the speed of a fluid occurs simultaneously with a decrease in&nbsp;<a title="Pressure" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pressure">pressure</a>&nbsp;or a decrease in the&nbsp;<a title="Fluid" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fluid">fluid</a>'s&nbsp;<a title="Potential energy" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potential_energy">potential energy</a>.</dd>
<dt id="bessel_function" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Bessel function" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bessel_function">Bessel function</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Bessel functions, first defined by the mathematician&nbsp;<a title="Daniel Bernoulli" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Daniel_Bernoulli">Daniel Bernoulli</a>&nbsp;and then generalized by&nbsp;<a title="Friedrich Bessel" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich_Bessel">Friedrich Bessel</a>, are canonical solutions&nbsp;<span class="texhtml"><em>y</em>(<em>x</em>)</span>&nbsp;of Bessel's&nbsp;<a title="Differential equation" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_equation">differential equation</a>.<img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="3.png" alt="{\displaystyle x^{2}{\frac {d^{2}y}{dx^{2}}}+x{\frac {dy}{dx}}+\left(x^{2}-\alpha ^{2}\right)y=0}" /><br /> for an arbitrary&nbsp;<a title="Complex number" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_number">complex number</a>&nbsp;<span class="texhtml mvar">&alpha;</span>, the&nbsp;<em>order</em>&nbsp;of the Bessel function. Although&nbsp;<span class="texhtml mvar">&alpha;</span>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<span class="texhtml">&minus;<em>&alpha;</em></span>&nbsp;produce the same differential equation, it is conventional to define different Bessel functions for these two values in such a way that the Bessel functions are mostly smooth functions of&nbsp;<span class="texhtml mvar">&alpha;</span>. The most important cases are when&nbsp;<span class="texhtml mvar">&alpha;</span>&nbsp;is an&nbsp;<a title="Integer" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integer">integer</a>&nbsp;or&nbsp;<a title="Half-integer" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Half-integer">half-integer</a>. Bessel functions for integer&nbsp;<span class="texhtml mvar">&alpha;</span>&nbsp;are also known as cylinder functions or the&nbsp;<strong><a title="Cylindrical harmonics" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cylindrical_harmonics">cylindrical harmonics</a></strong>&nbsp;because they appear in the solution to&nbsp;<a title="Laplace's equation" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laplace%27s_equation">Laplace's equation</a>&nbsp;in&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Cylindrical coordinates" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cylindrical_coordinates">cylindrical coordinates</a>.&nbsp;<strong><a class="mw-redirect" title="Spherical Bessel functions" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spherical_Bessel_functions">Spherical Bessel functions</a></strong>&nbsp;with half-integer&nbsp;<span class="texhtml mvar">&alpha;</span>&nbsp;are obtained when the&nbsp;<a title="Helmholtz equation" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helmholtz_equation">Helmholtz equation</a>&nbsp;is solved in&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Spherical coordinates" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spherical_coordinates">spherical coordinates</a>.</dd>
</dl>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt id="beta_decay" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Beta decay" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beta_decay">beta decay</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In&nbsp;<a title="Nuclear physics" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_physics">nuclear physics</a>, beta decay (<em>&beta;</em>-decay) is a type of&nbsp;<a title="Radioactive decay" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radioactive_decay">radioactive decay</a>&nbsp;in which a&nbsp;<a title="Beta particle" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beta_particle">beta particle</a>&nbsp;(fast energetic&nbsp;<a title="Electron" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron">electron</a>&nbsp;or&nbsp;<a title="Positron" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Positron">positron</a>) is emitted from an&nbsp;<a title="Atomic nucleus" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_nucleus">atomic nucleus</a>, transforming the original&nbsp;<a title="Nuclide" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclide">nuclide</a>&nbsp;to its&nbsp;<a title="Isobar (nuclide)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isobar_(nuclide)">isobar</a>.</dd>
<dt id="beta_particle" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Beta particle" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beta_particle">beta particle</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A high-energy, high-speed&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electron"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electron">electron</span></a>&nbsp;or&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#positron"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;positron">positron</span></a>&nbsp;emitted by certain types of radioactive atomic nuclei.</dd>
<dt id="big_bang" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Big Bang" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_Bang">Big Bang</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The prevailing cosmological model that describes the early development of the&nbsp;<a title="Universe" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universe">Universe</a>.</dd>
<dt id="binding_energy" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Binding energy" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binding_energy">binding energy</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The mechanical energy required to disassemble a whole into separate parts. A bound system typically has a lower potential energy than the sum of its constituent parts.</dd>
<dt id="binomial_random_variable" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Binomial random variable" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binomial_random_variable">binomial random variable</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="biocatalysis" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Biocatalysis" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biocatalysis">biocatalysis</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="biophysics" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Biophysics" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biophysics">biophysics</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An interdisciplinary science using methods of and theories from physics to study&nbsp;<a title="Biology" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biology">biological</a>&nbsp;systems.</dd>
<dt id="black_body" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Black body" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_body">black body</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A hypothetical idealized physical body that completely absorbs all incident&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electromagnetic_radiation"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</span></a>, regardless of&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#frequency"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;frequency">frequency</span></a>&nbsp;or&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#angle_of_incidence"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;angle of incidence">angle of incidence</span></a>. Perfect black bodies are imagined as substitutes for actual physical bodies in many theoretical discussions of&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#thermodynamics"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;thermodynamics">thermodynamics</span></a>, and the construction of nearly perfect black bodies in the real world remains a topic of interest for materials engineers. Contrast&nbsp;<em><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#white_body"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;white body">white body</span></a></em>.</dd>
<dt id="black-body_radiation" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Black-body radiation" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black-body_radiation">black-body radiation</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The type of electromagnetic radiation within or surrounding a body in thermodynamic equilibrium with its environment, or emitted by a&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#black_body"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;black body">black body</span></a>&nbsp;(an opaque and non-reflective body) held at constant, uniform temperature. The radiation has a specific spectrum and intensity that depends only on the temperature of the body.</dd>
<dt id="block_and_tackle" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Block and tackle" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Block_and_tackle">block and tackle</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A system of two or more&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#pulley"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;pulley">pulleys</span></a>&nbsp;with a rope or cable threaded between them, usually used to lift or pull heavy loads.</dd>
<dt id="bohr_model" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Bohr model" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bohr_model">Bohr model</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="boiling_point" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Boiling point" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boiling_point">boiling point</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#temperature"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;temperature">temperature</span></a>&nbsp;at which a&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#liquid"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;liquid">liquid</span></a>&nbsp;undergoes a phase change into a&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#gas"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;gas">gas</span></a>; the vapour pressure of liquid and gas are equal at this temperature.</dd>
<dt id="boiling_point_elevation" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Boiling point elevation" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boiling_point_elevation">boiling point elevation</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Describes the phenomenon that the&nbsp;<a title="Boiling point" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boiling_point">boiling point</a>&nbsp;of a&nbsp;<a title="Liquid" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liquid">liquid</a>&nbsp;(a&nbsp;<a title="Solvent" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solvent">solvent</a>) will be higher when another compound is added, meaning that a&nbsp;<a title="Solution" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solution">solution</a>&nbsp;has a higher boiling point than a pure solvent. This happens whenever a non-volatile solute, such as a salt, is added to a pure solvent, such as water. The boiling point can be measured accurately using an&nbsp;<a title="Ebullioscope" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ebullioscope">ebullioscope</a>.</dd>
<dt id="boltzmann_constant" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Boltzmann constant" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boltzmann_constant">Boltzmann constant</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A physical constant relating the average&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#kinetic_energy"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;kinetic energy">kinetic energy</span></a>&nbsp;of the particles in a&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#gas"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;gas">gas</span></a>&nbsp;with the&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#temperature"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;temperature">temperature</span></a>&nbsp;of the gas. It is the gas constant R divided by the Avogadro constant NA.</dd>
<dt id="bose&ndash;einstein_condensate_(bec)" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Bose&ndash;Einstein condensate" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bose%E2%80%93Einstein_condensate">Bose&ndash;Einstein condensate</a>&nbsp;(BEC)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="boson" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Boson" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boson">boson</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#particle"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;particle">particle</span></a>&nbsp;that behaves according to&nbsp;<a title="Bose&ndash;Einstein statistics" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bose%E2%80%93Einstein_statistics">Bose&ndash;Einstein statistics</a>&nbsp;and possesses integer&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#spin"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;spin">spin</span></a>. Bosons include&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#elementary_particle"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;elementary particle">elementary particles</span></a>&nbsp;such as&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#photon"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;photon">photons</span></a>,&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#gluon"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;gluon">gluons</span></a>,&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#w_and_Z_bosons"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;W and Z bosons">W and Z bosons</span></a>,&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#higgs_boson"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;Higgs boson">Higgs bosons</span></a>, and the hypothetical&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#graviton"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;graviton">graviton</span></a>, as well as certain&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#composite_particle"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;composite particle">composite particles</span></a>&nbsp;such as&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#meson"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;meson">mesons</span></a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#stable_nuclide"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;stable nuclide">stable nuclides</span></a>&nbsp;of even&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#mass_number"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;mass number">mass number</span></a>. Bosons constitute one of two main classes of particles, the other being&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#fermion"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;fermion">fermions</span></a>. Unlike fermions, there is no limit to the number of bosons that can occupy the same&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#quantum_state"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;quantum state">quantum state</span></a>.</dd>
<dt id="boyle's_law" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Boyle's law" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boyle%27s_law">Boyle's law</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The volume of a given mass of a gas at constant temperature is inversely proportional to the pressure.</dd>
<dt id="bra&ndash;ket_notation" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Bra&ndash;ket notation" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bra%E2%80%93ket_notation">Bra&ndash;ket notation</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="bragg's_law" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Bragg's law" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bragg%27s_law">Bragg's law</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="bremsstrahlung" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Bremsstrahlung" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bremsstrahlung">bremsstrahlung</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Radiation emitted by the&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#acceleration"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;acceleration">acceleration</span></a>&nbsp;of unbound charged particles.</dd>
<dt id="brewster's_angle" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Brewster's angle" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brewster%27s_angle">Brewster's angle</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="british_thermal_unit_(btu)" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="British thermal unit" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_thermal_unit">british thermal unit</a>&nbsp;(btu)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An Imperial unit of&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#energy"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;energy">energy</span></a>&nbsp;defined as the amount of energy needed to heat one pound of water by one degree Fahrenheit; 1 btu is equal to about 1055&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#joule"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;joule">joules</span></a>. In scientific contexts the btu has largely been replaced by the SI unit of energy, the joule.</dd>
<dt id="brittleness" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Brittleness" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brittleness">brittleness</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The tendency of a material to break without significant&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#plasticity"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;plasticity">plastic</span></a>&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#deformation"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;deformation">deformation</span></a>&nbsp;when subjected to&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#stress"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;stress">stress</span></a>. Brittle materials absorb relatively little energy prior to fracture, even those of high strength. Breaking is often accompanied by a snapping sound.</dd>
<dt id="brownian_motion" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Brownian motion" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brownian_motion">Brownian motion</a></dfn></dt>
</dl>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><span class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also called&nbsp;<strong>pedesis</strong>.</span></p>
<dl class="glossary">
<dd class="glossary">The presumably random movement of particles suspended in a fluid (liquid or gas) resulting from their bombardment by the fast-moving atoms or molecules in the gas or liquid.</dd>
<dt id="bulk_modulus" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Bulk modulus" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bulk_modulus">Bulk modulus</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A measure of a substance's resistance to uniform compression defined as the ratio of the infinitesimal pressure increase to the resulting relative decrease of the volume. Its base unit is the&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#pascal"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;pascal">pascal</span></a>.</dd>
<dt id="buoyancy" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Buoyancy" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buoyancy">buoyancy</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An upward&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#force"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;force">force</span></a>&nbsp;exerted by a fluid that opposes the weight of an immersed object.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span id="C" class="mw-headline">C</span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt id="calculus" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Calculus" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calculus">calculus</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A branch of&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#mathematics"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;mathematics">mathematics</span></a>&nbsp;that studies change and has two major sub-fields:&nbsp;<a title="Differential calculus" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_calculus">differential calculus</a>&nbsp;(concerning rates of change and slopes of curves), and&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Integral calculus" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral_calculus">integral calculus</a>&nbsp;(concerning accumulation of quantities and the areas under and between curves). These two branches are related to each other by the fundamental theorem of calculus.</dd>
<dt id="capacitance" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Capacitance" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capacitance">capacitance</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Is the ratio of the change in an&nbsp;<a title="Electric charge" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_charge">electric charge</a>&nbsp;in a system to the corresponding change in its&nbsp;<a title="Electric potential" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_potential">electric potential</a>. There are two closely related notions of capacitance:&nbsp;<em>self capacitance</em>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<em>mutual capacitance</em>. Any object that can be electrically charged exhibits&nbsp;<em>self capacitance</em>. A material with a large self capacitance holds more electric charge at a given&nbsp;<a title="Voltage" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voltage">voltage</a>&nbsp;than one with low capacitance. The notion of&nbsp;<em>mutual capacitance</em>&nbsp;is particularly important for understanding the operations of the&nbsp;<a title="Capacitor" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capacitor">capacitor</a>, one of the three elementary&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Nonlinear circuit" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nonlinear_circuit">linear</a>&nbsp;electronic components (along with&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Resistors" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Resistors">resistors</a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Inductors" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inductors">inductors</a>).</dd>
<dt id="capacitive_reactance" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Capacitive reactance" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capacitive_reactance">capacitive reactance</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A capacitor consists of two&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Electric conduction" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_conduction">conductors</a>&nbsp;separated by an&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical insulation" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_insulation">insulator</a>, also known as a&nbsp;<a title="Dielectric" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dielectric">dielectric</a>.&nbsp;<em>Capacitive reactance</em>&nbsp;is an opposition to the change of voltage across an element. Capacitive reactance <em>X<sub>C</sub></em> is&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Inversely proportional" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inversely_proportional">inversely proportional</a>&nbsp;to the signal&nbsp;<a title="Frequency" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frequency">frequency</a>&nbsp;<em>f</em>&nbsp;(or&nbsp;<a title="Angular frequency" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angular_frequency">angular frequency</a>&nbsp;&omega;) and the&nbsp;<a title="Capacitance" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capacitance">capacitance</a>&nbsp;<em><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y">C</span></span></em>.</dd>
<dt id="carnot_cycle" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Carnot cycle" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carnot_cycle">Carnot cycle</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Is a theoretical ideal&nbsp;<a title="Thermodynamic cycle" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermodynamic_cycle">thermodynamic cycle</a>&nbsp;proposed by French physicist&nbsp;<a title="Nicolas L&eacute;onard Sadi Carnot" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nicolas_L%C3%A9onard_Sadi_Carnot">Sadi Carnot</a>&nbsp;in 1824 and expanded upon by others in the 1830s and 1840s. It provides an upper limit on the efficiency that any classical thermodynamic engine can achieve during the conversion of&nbsp;<a title="Heat" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heat">heat</a>&nbsp;into&nbsp;<a title="Work (physics)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Work_(physics)">work</a>, or conversely, the efficiency of a&nbsp;<a title="Refrigeration" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Refrigeration">refrigeration</a>&nbsp;system in creating a temperature difference by the application of work to the system. It is not an actual thermodynamic cycle but is a theoretical construct.</dd>
<dt id="cartesian_coordinate_system" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Cartesian coordinate system" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system">Cartesian coordinate system</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Is a&nbsp;<a title="Coordinate system" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coordinate_system">coordinate system</a>&nbsp;that specifies each&nbsp;<a title="Point (geometry)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Point_(geometry)">point</a>&nbsp;uniquely in a&nbsp;<a title="Plane (geometry)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plane_(geometry)">plane</a>&nbsp;by a set of&nbsp;<a title="Number" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Number">numerical</a>&nbsp;coordinates, which are the&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Positive and negative numbers" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Positive_and_negative_numbers">signed</a>&nbsp;distances to the point from two fixed&nbsp;<a title="Perpendicular" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perpendicular">perpendicular</a>&nbsp;oriented lines, measured in the same&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Unit length" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_length">unit of length</a>. Each reference line is called a&nbsp;<em>coordinate axis</em>&nbsp;or just&nbsp;<em>axis</em>&nbsp;(plural&nbsp;<em>axes</em>) of the system, and the point where they meet is its&nbsp;<em><a title="Origin (mathematics)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Origin_(mathematics)">origin</a></em>, at ordered pair&nbsp;<span class="nowrap">(0, 0)</span>. The coordinates can also be defined as the positions of the&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Orthogonal projection" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orthogonal_projection">perpendicular projections</a>&nbsp;of the point onto the two axes, expressed as signed distances from the origin.</dd>
<dt id="cathode" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Cathode" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cathode">cathode</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The electrode through which a conventional&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#current"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;current">electric current</span></a>&nbsp;flows out of a polarized electrical device; the direction of current flow is, by convention, opposite to the direction of&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electron"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electron">electron</span></a>&nbsp;flow, and so electrons flow&nbsp;<em>into</em>&nbsp;the cathode. In a&nbsp;<a title="Galvanic cell" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galvanic_cell">galvanic cell</a>, the cathode is the positive terminal or pole which accepts electrons flowing from the external part of an&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electrical_circuit"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electrical circuit">electrical circuit</span></a>. However, in an&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electrolytic_cell"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electrolytic cell">electrolytic cell</span></a>, the cathode is the wire or plate having excess&nbsp;<em>negative</em>&nbsp;charge, so named because positively charged&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cation"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;cation">cations</span></a>&nbsp;tend to move towards it. Contrast&nbsp;<em><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#anode"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;anode">anode</span></a></em>.</dd>
<dt id="cathode_ray" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Cathode ray" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cathode_ray">cathode ray</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="cation" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Cation" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cation">cation</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A positively charged&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#ion"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;ion">ion</span></a>. Contrast&nbsp;<em><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#anion"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;anion">anion</span></a></em>.</dd>
<dt id="celestial_mechanics" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Celestial mechanics" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celestial_mechanics">celestial mechanics</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="celsius_scale" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Celsius scale" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celsius_scale">Celsius scale</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A scale and unit of measurement of&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#temperature"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;temperature">temperature</span></a>, also known as Centigrade.</dd>
<dt id="center_of_curvature" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Center of curvature" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Center_of_curvature">center of curvature</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="center_of_gravity" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Center of gravity" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Center_of_gravity">center of gravity</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The point in a body around which the resultant torque due to gravity forces vanish. Near the surface of the earth, where the gravity acts downward as a parallel force field, the center of gravity and the center of mass are the same.</dd>
<dt id="center_of_mass" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Center of mass" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Center_of_mass">center of mass</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A distribution of mass in space is the unique point where the weighted relative position of the distributed mass sums to zero.</dd>
<dt id="center_of_pressure" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Center of pressure (fluid mechanics)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Center_of_pressure_(fluid_mechanics)">center of pressure</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="centigrade" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Centigrade (temperature)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centigrade_(temperature)">centigrade</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A scale and unit of measurement of&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#temperature"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;temperature">temperature</span></a>, also known as Celsius.</dd>
<dt id="central_force_motion" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Central force motion" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_force_motion">central force motion</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="central_limit_theorem" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Central limit theorem" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_limit_theorem">central limit theorem</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="centrifugal_force" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Centrifugal force" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centrifugal_force">centrifugal force</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The apparent outward force that draws a rotating body away from the centre of rotation. It is caused by the inertia of the body as the body's path is continually redirected.</dd>
<dt id="centripetal_force" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Centripetal force" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centripetal_force">centripetal force</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A force which keeps a body moving with a uniform speed along a circular path and is directed along the radius towards the centre.</dd>
<dt id="cgh_physics" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="CGh physics" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CGh_physics">cGh physics</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="chain_reaction" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Chain reaction" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chain_reaction">chain reaction</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A sequence of reactions in which a reactive product or byproduct causes additional similar reactions to take place.</dd>
<dt id="change_of_base_rule" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Change of base rule" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Change_of_base_rule">change of base rule</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="charge_carrier" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Charge carrier" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charge_carrier">charge carrier</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="chemical_physics" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Chemical physics" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemical_physics">chemical physics</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A branch of&nbsp;<a title="Chemistry" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemistry">chemistry</a>&nbsp;and physics that studies chemical processes from the point of view of physics by investigating physicochemical phenomena using techniques from atomic and molecular physics and condensed matter physics.</dd>
<dt id="chromatic_aberration" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Chromatic aberration" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chromatic_aberration">chromatic aberration</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="circular_motion" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Circular motion" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circular_motion">circular motion</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="classical_mechanics" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Classical mechanics" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_mechanics">classical mechanics</a></dfn></dt>
</dl>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><span class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also called&nbsp;<strong>Newtonian mechanics</strong>.</span></p>
<dl class="glossary">
<dd class="glossary">A sub-field of&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#mechanics"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;mechanics">mechanics</span></a>&nbsp;concerned with the set of&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Physical laws" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physical_laws">physical laws</a>&nbsp;describing the motion of bodies under the collective actions of a system of&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#force"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;force">forces</span></a>.</dd>
<dt id="coefficient_of_friction" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Friction" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friction#Coefficient_of_friction">coefficient of friction</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="coherence" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Coherence (physics)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coherence_(physics)">coherence</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="cohesion" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Cohesion (chemistry)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cohesion_(chemistry)">cohesion</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The tendency of similar particles or surfaces to cling to one another. Contrast&nbsp;<em><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#adhesion"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;adhesion">adhesion</span></a></em>.</dd>
<dt id="cold_fusion" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Cold fusion" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_fusion">cold fusion</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="complex_harmonic_motion" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Complex harmonic motion" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_harmonic_motion">complex harmonic motion</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="composite_particle" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Composite particle" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Composite_particle">composite particle</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="compton_scattering" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Compton scattering" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compton_scattering">Compton scattering</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="concave_lens" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Concave lens" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Concave_lens">concave lens</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="condensation_point" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Condensation point" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Condensation_point">condensation point</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="condensed_matter_physics" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Condensed matter physics" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Condensed_matter_physics">condensed matter physics</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A branch of physics that studies the physical properties of condensed phases of matter.</dd>
<dt id="conservation_of_momentum" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Momentum" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Momentum#Conservation">conservation of momentum</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="conservation_law" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Conservation law" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conservation_law">conservation law</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="constructive_interference" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Constructive interference" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constructive_interference">constructive interference</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="continuous_spectrum" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Continuous spectrum" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continuous_spectrum">continuous spectrum</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="continuum_mechanics" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Continuum mechanics" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continuum_mechanics">continuum mechanics</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="convection" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Convection" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convection">convection</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The transfer of heat by the actual transfer of matter.</dd>
<dt id="convex_lens" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Convex lens" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_lens">convex lens</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="coulomb_(c)" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Coulomb" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coulomb">coulomb</a>&nbsp;(C)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#sI"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;SI">SI</span></a>&nbsp;derived unit of&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electric_charge"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electric charge">electric charge</span></a>, defined as the charge transported by a constant&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electric_current"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electric current">current</span></a>&nbsp;of one&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#ampere"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;ampere">ampere</span></a>&nbsp;in one second.</dd>
<dt id="coulomb's_law" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Coulomb's law" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coulomb%27s_law">Coulomb's law</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="converging_lens" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Lens (optics)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lens_(optics)#Types_of_simple_lenses">converging lens</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="cosmic_background_radiation" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Cosmic background radiation" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmic_background_radiation">cosmic background radiation</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="covalent_bond" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Covalent bond" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Covalent_bond">covalent bond</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="creep" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Creep (deformation)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Creep_(deformation)">creep</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="crest" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Crest (physics)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crest_(physics)">crest</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The point on a&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#wave"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;wave">wave</span></a>&nbsp;with the maximum value or upward displacement within a cycle.</dd>
<dt id="crest_factor" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Crest factor" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crest_factor">crest factor</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="critical_angle" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Critical angle (optics)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Critical_angle_(optics)">critical angle</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="critical_mass" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Critical mass" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Critical_mass">critical mass</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The smallest amount of fissile material needed for a sustained nuclear&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#chain_reaction"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;chain reaction">chain reaction</span></a>.</dd>
<dt id="curie_temperature" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Curie temperature" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Curie_temperature">Curie temperature</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="current_density" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Current density" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Current_density">current density</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="current_length" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary">current length</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="curvilinear_motion" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Curvilinear motion" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Curvilinear_motion">curvilinear motion</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="cyclotron" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Cyclotron" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyclotron">cyclotron</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#particle_accelerator"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;particle accelerator">particle accelerator</span></a>&nbsp;in which&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Charged particles" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charged_particles">charged particles</a>&nbsp;accelerate outwards from the center along a spiral path.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span id="D" class="mw-headline">D</span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt id="dalton's_law" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Dalton's law" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dalton%27s_law">Dalton's law</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="damped_vibration" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Damped vibration" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Damped_vibration">damped vibration</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="damping" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Damping" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Damping">damping</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="darcy&ndash;weisbach_equation" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Darcy&ndash;Weisbach equation" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Darcy%E2%80%93Weisbach_equation">Darcy&ndash;Weisbach equation</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="dark_energy" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Dark energy" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dark_energy">dark energy</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="dark_matter" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Dark matter" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dark_matter">dark matter</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="dc_motor" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="DC motor" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DC_motor">DC motor</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A mechanically commutated electric motor powered by&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#direct_current"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;direct current">direct current</span></a>.</dd>
<dt id="decibel" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Decibel" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decibel">decibel</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="definite_integral" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Definite integral" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Definite_integral">definite integral</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="deflection" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Deflection (engineering)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deflection_(engineering)">deflection</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The degree to which a structural element is displaced under a load. It may refer to an angle or a distance.</dd>
<dt id="deformation" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Deformation (mechanics)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deformation_(mechanics)">deformation</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&nbsp;&nbsp;(mechanics)</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&nbsp;&nbsp;(engineering)</dd>
<dt id="density" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Density" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Density">density</a></dfn></dt>
</dl>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><span class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also called&nbsp;<strong>mass density</strong>.</span></p>
<dl class="glossary">
<dd class="glossary">A physical property of a substance defined as its&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#mass"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;mass">mass</span></a>&nbsp;per unit volume.</dd>
<dt id="derivative" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Derivative" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Derivative">derivative</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="desert" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Desert (particle physics)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Desert_(particle_physics)">desert</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="destructive_interference" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Destructive interference" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Destructive_interference">destructive interference</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="diamagnetism" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Diamagnetism" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diamagnetism">diamagnetism</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="dielectric" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Dielectric" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dielectric">dielectric</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="diffraction" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Diffraction" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diffraction">diffraction</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="direct_current_(dc)" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Direct current" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Direct_current">direct current</a>&nbsp;(DC)</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="dispersion" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Dispersion (optics)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dispersion_(optics)">dispersion</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="displacement_(fluid)" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Displacement (fluid)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Displacement_(fluid)">displacement (fluid)</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Occurs when an object is immersed in a fluid, pushing it out of the way and taking its place. The volume of the fluid displaced can then be measured, and from this the volume of the immersed object can be deduced (the volume of the immersed object will be exactly equal to the volume of the displaced fluid).</dd>
<dt id="displacement_(vector)" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Displacement (vector)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Displacement_(vector)">displacement (vector)</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The shortest distance from the initial to the final position of a point. Thus, it is the length of an imaginary straight path, typically distinct from the path actually travelled by.</dd>
<dt id="distance" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Distance" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Distance">distance</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A numerical description of how far apart objects are.</dd>
<dt id="drift_velocity" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Drift velocity" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drift_velocity">drift velocity</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="doppler_effect" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Doppler effect" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doppler_effect">Doppler effect</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The change in frequency of a wave (or other periodic event) for an observer moving relative to its source.The received frequency is higher (compared to the emitted frequency) during the approach, it is identical at the instant of passing by, and it is lower during the recession.</dd>
<dt id="drag" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Drag (physics)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drag_(physics)">drag</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Forces which act on a solid object in the direction of the relative fluid flow velocity. Unlike other resistive forces, such as dry friction, which is nearly independent of velocity, drag forces depend on velocity.</dd>
<dt id="ductility" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Ductility" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ductility">ductility</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A solid material's ability to deform under tensile stress; this is often characterized by the material's ability to be stretched into a wire.</dd>
<dt id="dynamics" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Dynamics (mechanics)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dynamics_(mechanics)">dynamics</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The branch of&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#classical_mechanics"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;classical mechanics">classical mechanics</span></a>&nbsp;that studies&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#force"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;force">forces</span></a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#torque"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;torque">torques</span></a>&nbsp;and their effects on&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#motion"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;motion">motion</span></a>, as opposed to&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#kinematics"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;kinematics">kinematics</span></a>, which studies motion without reference to these forces.</dd>
<dt id="dyne" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Dyne" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dyne">dyne</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
</dl>
<h2><span id="E" class="mw-headline">E</span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt id="econophysics" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Econophysics" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Econophysics">econophysics</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="elastic_collision" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Elastic collision" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elastic_collision">elastic collision</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="elastic_energy" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Elastic energy" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elastic_energy">elastic energy</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="elastic_instability" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Elastic instability" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elastic_instability">elastic instability</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="elastic_modulus" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Elastic modulus" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elastic_modulus">elastic modulus</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="elasticity" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Elasticity (physics)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elasticity_(physics)">elasticity</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The tendency of a material to return to its original shape after it is&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#deformation"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;deformation">deformed</span></a>.</dd>
<dt id="electric_charge" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Electric charge" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_charge">electric charge</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A physical property of&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#matter"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;matter">matter</span></a>&nbsp;that causes it to experience a&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#force"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;force">force</span></a>&nbsp;when near other electrically charged matter. There are two types of electric charge: positive and negative.</dd>
<dt id="electric_circuit" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Electric circuit" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_circuit">electric circuit</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electrical_network"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electrical network">electrical network</span></a>&nbsp;consisting of a closed loop, giving a return path for the&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#current"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;current">current</span></a>.</dd>
<dt id="electric_current" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Electric current" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_current">electric current</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A flow of&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electric_charge"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electric charge">electric charge</span></a>&nbsp;through a conductive medium.</dd>
<dt id="electric_displacement_field" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Electric displacement field" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_displacement_field">electric displacement field</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="electric_field" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Electric field" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_field">electric field</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The region of space surrounding electrically charged particles and time-varying magnetic fields. The electric field depicts the force exerted on other electrically charged objects by the electrically charged particle the field is surrounding.</dd>
<dt id="electric_field_gradient" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Electric field gradient" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_field_gradient">electric field gradient</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="electric_field_intensity" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Electric intensity" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_intensity">electric field intensity</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="electric_generator" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Electric generator" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_generator">electric generator</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="electric_motor" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Electric motor" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_motor">electric motor</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="electric_potential" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Electric potential" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_potential">electric potential</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="electric_power" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Electric power" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_power">electric power</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The rate at which electric energy is transferred by an&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electric_circuit"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electric circuit">electric circuit</span></a>.</dd>
<dt id="electrical_conductor" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Electrical conductor" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_conductor">electrical conductor</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Any material which contains movable&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electric_charge"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electric charge">electric charges</span></a>&nbsp;and therefore can conduct an&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electric_current"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electric current">electric current</span></a>&nbsp;under the influence of an&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electric_field"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electric field">electric field</span></a>.</dd>
<dt id="electrical_insulator" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical insulator" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_insulator">electrical insulator</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Any material whose internal&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electric_charge"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electric charge">electric charges</span></a>&nbsp;do not flow freely and which therefore does not conduct an&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electric_current"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electric current">electric current</span></a>&nbsp;under the influence of an&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electric_field"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electric field">electric field</span></a>.</dd>
<dt id="electrical_potential_energy" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical potential energy" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_potential_energy">electrical potential energy</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="electrical_and_electronics_engineering" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical and electronics engineering" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_and_electronics_engineering">electrical and electronics engineering</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary"></dd>
<dt id="electrical_network" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Electrical network" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_network">electrical network</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An interconnection of electrical elements such as resistors, inductors, capacitors, voltage sources, current sources and switches.</dd>
<dt id="electrical_resistance" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical resistance" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_resistance">electrical resistance</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The opposition to the passage of an&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#electric_current"><span id="" class="glossary-link-internal" title="See entry on this page at &sect;electric current">electric current</span></a>&nbsp;through an electrical element.</dd>
<dt id="electricity" class="glossary"><dfn class="glossary"><a title="Electricity" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electricity">electricity</a></dfn></dt>
